Hong Kong: Agency (1960-1970)

Christian or Pro-Christian Leaders

  1. John Gilbert Hyndley Baker (Wickeri 2021)

    John Gilbert Hyndley Baker (1910-1986) was the eighth bishop of the Hong Kong and Macau Diocese of the Anglican Church (1966-1981) and the first bishop of Hong Kong elected to the position.

    Born in Portsmouth in October 1910, he entered Christ Church, Oxford, in 1929, where he joined the Student Christian Movement. After graduating in 1932, he worked as a staff member for the Student Christian Movement at the University of London. Inspired by Bishop Ronald Hall, he went to China in late 1934. In 1935, he was ordained as a deacon by Hall at the Church of Our Saviour in Guangzhou and as a priest the following year. Baker taught at Trinity College and Lingnan University in Guangzhou and assisted in refugee relief during the city‚Äôs fall in 1938. From 1939 to 1945, he worked at Wen Lin Tang in Kunming, Yunnan, focusing on student ministry.

    After the war, he studied at Union Theological Seminary in New York in 1945 and returned to China in 1947 as chaplain of St. John‚Äôs University in Shanghai, while also teaching at Central Theological Seminary. In 1949, he moved to Guangzhou to teach at Lingnan University and the Canton Union Theological College. In January 1951, he left China, ending his missionary service.

    After leaving China, Baker served as a rector in the United States and as General Secretary of the United Society for the Propagation of the Gospel in England. In 1966, he became director of Tao Fong Shan Christian Centre in Hong Kong. That same year, following the resignation of Bishop de Blank due to health issues, Baker was elected as the Bishop of the Hong Kong and Macau Diocese on September 19 and was consecrated in December. In his inaugural address, he emphasized three priorities: promoting ecumenism, addressing the relationship between Christianity and social service, and strengthening ties with churches in China and Southeast Asia.

    During his tenure, Baker actively responded to social issues following the 1966 and 1967 riots in Hong Kong. In 1970, the diocese launched a five-year plan. By 1974, the Anglican Church in Hong Kong was running 46 primary schools, 22 secondary schools, and 14 social service units. A significant milestone was the ordination of the first women priests in the worldwide Anglican Communion‚ÄîJoyce Bennet and Li Tim-Oi‚Äîapproved by the Anglican Consultative Council in 1971.

    In January 1980, Baker was granted a visa to visit China, including Shenzhen, Guangzhou, and Fuzhou, where he reunited with former colleagues. In 1981, he retired at 70, succeeded by the first Chinese bishop of the Hong Kong and Macau Diocese, Peter Kwong.

    In 1984, an Anglican school in Yuen Long was named ‚ÄúBishop Baker Secondary School‚Äù in recognition of his contributions. Baker passed away in 1986.

    ‚Äî

    1. Hans Ltuz (Yiu 2024; Mak 2024; Alee 2023)

      Hans Ltuz (born 1936) is a Swiss missionary born into a Christian family. Inspired during his high school years, he committed himself to ministry. After completing theological training in 1964, he spent a year in France living with a worker-priest and participating in industrial mission efforts. During this time, he worked in a glass factory, deepening his understanding of workers‚Äô needs. Returning to Switzerland, he served in the Geneva Diocese before joining the Basel Mission. In 1968, he was assigned to Hong Kong for labor mission work.

      Arriving in Hong Kong after the 1967 riots, Ltuz recognized the importance of labor ministry. He joined the Christian Industrial Committee, advocating for labor law reforms to improve worker welfare. In 1977, he helped establish community labor churches, sharing faith with workers. His Labor Sunday sermons and prayers were later compiled into The Gospel for Workers.

      In 1971, he co-founded the Society for Community Organisation (SoCO) to support marginalized communities and advocate for their rights. In the 1970s, he addressed human rights issues, assisting in the establishment of Amnesty International‚Äôs Hong Kong branch in 1976. In 1988, he founded the Hong Kong Human Rights Commission and served as its chairman.

      From 1992 to 1997, Ltuz served as Asia Secretary for the Basel Mission, managing church partnerships in Indonesia, Malaysia, and Taiwan while supporting education, healthcare, and social services in tribal and rural areas. He also frequently visited churches in Guangdong Province.

      In November 2023, the University of Basel‚Äôs Faculty of Theology awarded Ltuz an honorary doctorate. He returned to Switzerland in March 2024.

      ‚Äî

      1. Lee Ching Chee (Lee 2000; Alee 2013; Christian Weekly 1966a)

        Lee Ching Chee (born 1933) was born in Hong Kong and fled to Guangzhou during the Japanese occupation, completing primary school there. She returned to Hong Kong in 1946, graduating from Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School in 1950 and from Grantham College of Education in 1953. She began her teaching career at St. Stephen‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College and, in 1954, returned to Ying Wa as a teacher after baptism in June.

        In 1958-59, Lee studied theology at Selly Oak Colleges, Birmingham, before returning to Ying Wa for five more years of teaching. In 1964-66, she pursued theological studies again in the UK. Upon returning to Hong Kong, she was ordained in October 1966 as the first female pastor of the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China.

        From 1966 to 1977, Lee served as the chaplain at Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School, also acting as vice-principal from 1972 to 1977. She represented the church at the World Council of Churches‚Äô Fourth Assembly, where she was elected to the Central Executive Committee. In 1977, she became the Mission Education Secretary for the Council for World Mission, visiting churches across Africa, India, the South Pacific, and Europe, including areas like South Africa, Northern Ireland, and Myanmar, often under curfews.

        Returning to Hong Kong in 1981, Lee served as Deputy General Secretary of the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China and held leadership roles in its schools until her retirement in December 1997.

        Lee was deeply involved in ecumenical work, serving in various leadership roles within the Hong Kong Christian Council from the 1960s onward. She published books including Half the Sky: The Status of Women in Churches (1993), Straightforward Words (1993), Collected Writings of Ching Chee (1998), and My Recollections (2000).

        1. David C. P. Lew (1929‚Äì1998)

          (Lew, 1968, 1970, 1972; Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union, 1998; Lee, 2014: 99-102)

          David C. P. Lew, born in Zhongshan, Guangdong, was originally a member of the Guangdong Chinese Christian Church Fujitang in Shanghai. He moved to Hong Kong following the regime change in China. In 1951, he dedicated himself to ministry during a summer camp held by the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China. In 1952, he enrolled at Trinity Theological College in Singapore and graduated in 1955. Ordained by the Church of Christ in China in 1959, he served as the pastor of Kowloon Church and held positions as vice-chairman and head of the publication department of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union. From 1968 to 1977, he served as the General Secretary of the Union.

          David Lew was deeply concerned about social issues. After the 1967 riots in Hong Kong, he wrote an article titled New Directions for Churches in Hong Kong‚Äôs Rapidly Changing Society. In it, he criticized churches for becoming ‚Äúsmall organizations serving only their members‚Äô spiritual and physical needs,‚Äù while remaining indifferent to societal issues. He urged churches to address labor, housing, and unemployment issues, submit memorandums to the government, and advocate for a society that aligns more closely with the principles of the Kingdom of Heaven.

          In a 1972 report as General Secretary of the Union, Lew again criticized churches for ignoring societal ‚Äúsins, darkness, and violence,‚Äù while also cautioning against abandoning gospel principles in the pursuit of social reform. He believed that both extremes failed to effectively witness to the truth of Christ. He emphasized the importance of expressing Christian views to the government while practicing the principle of ‚Äúbeing in the world but not of the world.‚Äù

          Writing under the pen name ‚ÄúAmos‚Äù in the Christian Weekly, Lew authored the column Reflections at the City Gate. For instance, in 1970, he foresaw that Hong Kong‚Äôs era of reliance on overseas aid for refugees would become a thing of the past.

          In 1975, when Billy Graham conducted his first evangelistic crusade in Hong Kong, Lew served as General Secretary of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union and as the campaign‚Äôs Executive Secretary.

          After stepping down in 1977, he worked for Kirin Press before moving to Toronto in 1981, where he became the Senior Pastor of the Toronto Chinese United Church. In the early 1990s, he helped establish the Toronto Chinese Christian Churches Union. He retired in 1994 and passed away in January 1998.

          ‚Äî

          1. Yung Chi-tung (1908‚Äì1987)

            (CUHK, 1987; Yung, 1960)

            Yung Chi-tung, born in Zhuhai, Guangdong, and raised in Hong Kong, was the son of Yung Hing-kiu, one of the Chinese students sent to study in the United States during the Qing Dynasty under the program organized by Yung Wing. After graduating from Lingnan School in Guangzhou, Yung studied biology at Tsinghua University, earning his degree in 1929. He stayed on as a teaching assistant and later pursued advanced studies at the University of Chicago, where he earned his Ph.D. in 1937.

            Returning to China, Yung taught at Northwest University and later at Lingnan University in Guangzhou, where he held various positions, including department chair, acting dean of the Faculty of Science, acting registrar, and acting president.

            In 1951, Yung moved to Hong Kong and joined the Department of Biology at the University of Hong Kong. In 1959, he became a board member of Chung Chi College and was appointed its third president in 1960. During his tenure, Chung Chi College prepared to join The Chinese University of Hong Kong (CUHK). When CUHK was established in 1963, Chung Chi became a founding college, and Yung also served as CUHK‚Äôs vice-chancellor. Additionally, he became the founding dean of Chung Chi Theological Seminary in 1963.

            Yung retired in 1975 and was named honorary president by the Chung Chi College Board. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1987.

            Before Chung Chi joined CUHK, Yung emphasized that the newly established university would operate as a ‚Äúfederated university,‚Äù allowing member colleges to retain their autonomy. He believed that Chung Chi College, which means ‚Äúto revere Christ,‚Äù should carry forward the traditions of the 13 Christian universities in China while adapting to new circumstances. He sought to integrate Christian values within the university structure.

            In recognition of his contributions to higher education in Hong Kong, Yung was awarded honorary Doctor of Laws degrees by the University of Hong Kong (1961) and CUHK (1975). He was also named a Fellow of the Royal Society for the Encouragement of Arts, Manufactures, and Commerce in 1972. In 2006, the new chapel building of Chung Chi Theological Seminary was named the ‚ÄúYung Chi-tung Memorial Building‚Äù in honor of his contributions to Christian education.

            ‚Äî

            1. Wu Ming Chieh (1916‚Äì1990)

              (Wu Yu Kui, 1996: 22-26)

              Wu Ming Chieh, born in Henan Province, became a Christian in 1943 and entered Hubei Shakou Lutheran Theological Seminary in 1946. In 1948, due to the Chinese Civil War, the seminary relocated to Tao Fong Shan in Hong Kong, where Wu arrived with faculty and students in December. After graduating in 1950, he served as an assistant preacher at the Lutheran Mandarin Church in Kowloon and was ordained as a pastor in November

  1. In 1952, Wu began mission work in Yuen Long, where he established a Lutheran Church.

    In 1959, he became president of the Lutheran Bible Seminary. In 1961, he was elected Supervisor of the Hong Kong Lutheran Church at its 8th General Assembly, a position he held through multiple terms, including in 1963, 1965, and 1969. Under his leadership, the church developed a seven-year plan for self-sufficiency, gradually reducing financial aid from overseas by one-seventh each year until achieving self-support in 1969.

    In 1968, the Lutheran Bible Seminary merged with another institution to form the Lutheran Theological Seminary, where Wu served as vice president. In 1970, he received an honorary Doctor of Theology degree from Pacific Lutheran University in the United States.

    During the 1970s, Wu continued his leadership roles, including representing the Lutheran Church in founding the Lutheran Theological Seminary of Hong Kong in collaboration with other denominations in 1976. He also chaired the organizing committee of the World Chinese Evangelical Fellowship in Hong Kong.

    In 1981, Wu retired as Supervisor but was named Honorary Lifetime Supervisor and Honorary Professor at the Lutheran Theological Seminary. He passed away in Hong Kong in September 1990.

    1968Âπ¥Ôºå‰ø°Áæ©ËÅñÁ∂ìÂ≠∏Èô¢Ëàá‰ø°Áæ©Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢Âêà‰ΩµÔºåÂê≥Âá∫‰ªªÂâØÈô¢Èï∑„ÄÇ1970Âπ¥ÔºåÁç≤ÁæéÂúãÂ§™Âπ≥Ê¥ã‰ø°Áæ©Â§ßÂ≠∏È†íÊéàÊ¶ÆË≠ΩÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÂçöÂ£´Â≠∏‰Ωç„ÄÇ‰∏ÉÂçÅÂπ¥‰ª£Ôºå‰ªñÂÖàÂæåÊñº1973Âπ¥„ÄÅ1979Âπ¥Ë¢´ÈÅ∏ÁÇ∫Áõ£Áù£„ÄÇÊúüÈñì‰ªñ‰ª£Ë°®‰ø°Áæ©ÊúÉËàáÁ¶ÆË≥¢ÊúÉÂèäÂ¥áÁúüÊúÉ‰ª£Ë°®ÔºåÁ∞ΩÁΩ≤ÊàêÁ´ã‰ø°Áæ©ÂÆóÊïôÊúÉËÅØÂêàËààËæ®Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÂçîË≠∞Ôºà1976ÔºâÔºåÂú®Ê≠§ÂçîË≠∞‰∏ãÔºåÁµÑÊàêÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰ø°Áæ©ÂÆóÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢„ÄÇ‰ªñÂèàÊì¨ÂÆöÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰ø°Áæ©ÊúÉÂÖ´ÂçÅÂπ¥‰ª£Âª∫Á´ãÊïôÊúÉÁ≠ñÁï•Êñá‰ª∂Ôºå‰∏¶Ë®Ç‰∏ãÊïôÁâß‰∫∫Âì°ÈÄÄ‰ºëÊ¢ù‰æã„ÄÇ1976Âπ¥‰∏ñÁïåËèØ‰∫∫Á¶èÈü≥‰∫ãÂ∑•ËÅØÁµ°‰∏≠ÂøÉÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÊàêÁ´ãÔºå‰ªñ‰ªªÂ∏∏ÂãôÂßîÂì°ÊúÉ‰∏ªÂ∏≠„ÄÇ1981Âπ¥Âç∏‰ªªÂæåÔºåË¢´Âßî‰ªªÁÇ∫Ê∞∏ÈÅ†Ê¶ÆË≠ΩÁõ£Áù£Âèä‰ø°Áæ©ÂÆóÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢Ê¶ÆË≠ΩÊïôÊéà„ÄÇ1990ÊÜê9ÊúàÔºåÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁóÖÈÄù„ÄÇ

    1. John Pao (1922‚Äì2013)

      (Pang 2014: 173-176)

      John Pao was born in Beijing in 1922 and converted to Christianity in

  2. In 1949, he pursued further theological education in the U.S., attending Wheaton College, Fuller Theological Seminary, Westminster Theological Seminary, and North Baptist Theological Seminary. He earned his Doctor of Theology in 1959 and was ordained as a pastor by the Evangelical Free Church of Wheaton that same year.

    In October 1959, Pao moved to Hong Kong, initially teaching at the Guangzhou Bible Institute. In 1961, he became the institute‚Äôs president. In 1965, the institute was renamed the Evangel Seminary, where Pao served as president until his retirement in 1985.

    Pao was dedicated to the service of the Evangelical Free Church of China, serving as chairman of the General Assembly (1971‚Äì1976) and as Supervisor from 1989 until his retirement in 1993, after which he was named Honorary Supervisor. In 1994, he moved to the U.S. to serve as president of the Christ Workers Theological Seminary in California, a position he held until 2000. In 2003, he returned to Hong Kong at the invitation of the Evangel Seminary to serve as ‚ÄúSpecial Appointed President‚Äù for a year.

    Pao authored several books, including The Armor of Truth, The Equipment of Life, and commentaries on Colossians, Romans, and Revelation, as well as Theology of the Bible and Christology. Between 1960 and 1995, he served as a speaker at the Hong Kong and Kowloon Bible Study and Evangelistic Conference ten times.

    ‚Äî

    1. Chan Hay Him (1934‚Äì2019)

      (Chan 2015; Fellowship of Evangelical Students, 2019)

      Chan Hay Him was born in Jieyang County, Guangdong, into a Christian family. At the age of 16, he moved to Hong Kong alone and attended Pui Ching Middle School. In July 1951, he experienced spiritual revival during a summer camp hosted by the Hong Kong Baptist Youth Fellowship. In 1954, during another camp, he responded to a call to ministry. After graduating from high school in 1955, he enrolled at the Hong Kong Baptist Theological Seminary and began attending and serving at the Hong Kong Chiu Chow Baptist Church.

      After graduating in 1960, Chan dedicated himself to student evangelism. In 1961, he became the first General Secretary of the Fellowship of Evangelical Students in Hong Kong, a position he held until 1974. In 1973, he pursued further studies in missions at Fuller Theological Seminary in the U.S., earning a Master‚Äôs degree. Chan also helped initiate the Breakthrough movement, suggesting its name.

      In 1975, Chan became the Student Counselor and Missions Lecturer at the China Graduate School of Theology. From 1977 to 1980, he served as General Secretary of Ambassadors for Christ in the U.S. and was ordained as a pastor in New York in November 1978. He later served as Senior Pastor at North York Chinese Baptist Church in Toronto, Canada (1980‚Äì1986, 1997‚Äì2000). From 1986 to 1996, he held two terms as General Secretary of the Chinese Coordination Centre of World Evangelism.

      Returning to Hong Kong in 2000, Chan served as Senior Pastor of the West Point Chiu Chow Baptist Church until his retirement in 2008. His publications include Why Doesn‚Äôt the Church Measure Up? (2008) and In These Years: God‚Äôs Revival Work and the Student Evangelism Movement (2015).

      ‚Äî

      1. Wong Tsok (1922‚Äì1995)

        (Hing 2014: 51-54)

        Wong Tsok, born in Xinhui, Guangdong, in March 1922, grew up in a rural non-Christian family. At age 11, he moved to Foshan, where he joined the Sunday School at the Wen Chang Sha Church of the Methodist Church, cultivating his Christian faith. He later became a Sunday School teacher. In 1940, he was baptized by Rev. Frank White.

        In 1944, Wong committed to becoming a pastor and enrolled at the Union Theological College of Lingnan University in Guangzhou. After graduating in 1948, he was assigned to the Hong Kong Methodist Church. Between 1948 and 1951, he served as pastor of the Kowloon Circuit and was responsible for constructing the new Kowloon Church and establishing a school.

        In 1952, Wong was formally accepted as a Methodist minister. However, due to political shifts, the Hong Kong Methodist Circuit declared its independence from British imperialism in 1951 and operated under the name ‚ÄúChinese Methodist Church in Hong Kong Circuit‚Äù until 1955, when Rev. Shearer officiated the ordination.

        In 1959, the Hong Kong Circuit was reorganized as the Hong Kong District under the British Methodist Mission, with Wong serving as District Superintendent. He became Chairman of the Hong Kong District in 1964, overseeing the construction of the North Point Church (1966) and discussing a union with the Methodist Church of China. When the Hong Kong Methodist Church was officially formed in 1975, Wong was elected as its first President, serving two terms until 1982.

        Wong was a strong advocate for Christian education, overseeing the establishment of several schools, including Methodist School (1951), Methodist College (1958), North Point Methodist School (1964), and Wa Ying College (1971).

        In 1984, Wong was awarded an MBE (Member of the Order of the British Empire) by the Hong Kong government in recognition of his contributions to education and religion. Wong retired in 1987 and passed away on September 26, 1995.

        ‚Äî

        1. Jackie Pullinger (1944‚Äì)

          (St. Stephen‚Äôs Society; Pullinger & Grieg, 1996)

          Jackie Pullinger was born in the United Kingdom in 1944. At age five, she decided she wanted to become a missionary. After graduating from the Royal College of Music, her first job was teaching music in England. However, she continued to feel a call to missionary work.

          In 1965, Pullinger sought opportunities to serve in Hong Kong, but her attempts to secure a position as a music teacher or through churches failed. Undeterred, she arrived in Hong Kong alone in 1966.

          Pullinger found work teaching music at a kindergarten in the Kowloon Walled City, a lawless area notorious for drug trafficking and triad activity. She established the Kowloon Walled City Youth Club, offering activities such as table tennis and camping trips for local youth. Gradually, young people accepted the gospel message, but many were not welcomed by local churches due to their appearance and gang affiliations.

          Pullinger began hosting gatherings and even housing addicts in her home, where she helped them overcome addiction through spiritual prayer. Despite facing hostility from triads, she expanded her work, establishing several counseling centers and founding the St. Stephen‚Äôs Society in 1974.

          In 1985, the Hong Kong government allowed her to set up ‚ÄúHome of Joy‚Äù in temporary housing, where marginalized groups, including addicts and the elderly, could worship and dine together. She also worked with Vietnamese refugees and managed a refugee camp upon the invitation of the UNHCR in 1994.

          Pullinger received an MBE (Member of the Order of the British Empire) in 1988 for her contributions to public welfare. In 2023, the University of Hong Kong awarded her an honorary Doctor of Social Sciences in recognition of her passionate service to society.

                1. John Gilbert Hyndley Baker (Wickeri 2021)

                  John Gilbert Hyndley Baker (1910-1986) was the eighth bishop of the Hong Kong and Macau Diocese of the Anglican Church (1966-1981) and the first bishop of Hong Kong elected to the position.

                  Born in Portsmouth in October 1910, he entered Christ Church, Oxford, in 1929, where he joined the Student Christian Movement. After graduating in 1932, he worked as a staff member for the Student Christian Movement at the University of London. Inspired by Bishop Ronald Hall, he went to China in late 1934. In 1935, he was ordained as a deacon by Hall at the Church of Our Saviour in Guangzhou and as a priest the following year. Baker taught at Trinity College and Lingnan University in Guangzhou and assisted in refugee relief during the city‚Äôs fall in 1938. From 1939 to 1945, he worked at Wen Lin Tang in Kunming, Yunnan, focusing on student ministry.

                  After the war, he studied at Union Theological Seminary in New York in 1945 and returned to China in 1947 as chaplain of St. John‚Äôs University in Shanghai, while also teaching at Central Theological Seminary. In 1949, he moved to Guangzhou to teach at Lingnan University and the Canton Union Theological College. In January 1951, he left China, ending his missionary service.

                  After leaving China, Baker served as a rector in the United States and as General Secretary of the United Society for the Propagation of the Gospel in England. In 1966, he became director of Tao Fong Shan Christian Centre in Hong Kong. That same year, following the resignation of Bishop de Blank due to health issues, Baker was elected as the Bishop of the Hong Kong and Macau Diocese on September 19 and was consecrated in December. In his inaugural address, he emphasized three priorities: promoting ecumenism, addressing the relationship between Christianity and social service, and strengthening ties with churches in China and Southeast Asia.

                  During his tenure, Baker actively responded to social issues following the 1966 and 1967 riots in Hong Kong. In 1970, the diocese launched a five-year plan. By 1974, the Anglican Church in Hong Kong was running 46 primary schools, 22 secondary schools, and 14 social service units. A significant milestone was the ordination of the first women priests in the worldwide Anglican Communion‚ÄîJoyce Bennet and Li Tim-Oi‚Äîapproved by the Anglican Consultative Council in 1971.

                  In January 1980, Baker was granted a visa to visit China, including Shenzhen, Guangzhou, and Fuzhou, where he reunited with former colleagues. In 1981, he retired at 70, succeeded by the first Chinese bishop of the Hong Kong and Macau Diocese, Peter Kwong.

                  In 1984, an Anglican school in Yuen Long was named ‚ÄúBishop Baker Secondary School‚Äù in recognition of his contributions. Baker passed away in 1986.

                  ‚Äî

                  1. Hans Ltuz (Yiu 2024; Mak 2024; Alee 2023)

                    Hans Ltuz (born 1936) is a Swiss missionary born into a Christian family. Inspired during his high school years, he committed himself to ministry. After completing theological training in 1964, he spent a year in France living with a worker-priest and participating in industrial mission efforts. During this time, he worked in a glass factory, deepening his understanding of workers‚Äô needs. Returning to Switzerland, he served in the Geneva Diocese before joining the Basel Mission. In 1968, he was assigned to Hong Kong for labor mission work.

                    Arriving in Hong Kong after the 1967 riots, Ltuz recognized the importance of labor ministry. He joined the Christian Industrial Committee, advocating for labor law reforms to improve worker welfare. In 1977, he helped establish community labor churches, sharing faith with workers. His Labor Sunday sermons and prayers were later compiled into The Gospel for Workers.

                    In 1971, he co-founded the Society for Community Organisation (SoCO) to support marginalized communities and advocate for their rights. In the 1970s, he addressed human rights issues, assisting in the establishment of Amnesty International‚Äôs Hong Kong branch in 1976. In 1988, he founded the Hong Kong Human Rights Commission and served as its chairman.

                    From 1992 to 1997, Ltuz served as Asia Secretary for the Basel Mission, managing church partnerships in Indonesia, Malaysia, and Taiwan while supporting education, healthcare, and social services in tribal and rural areas. He also frequently visited churches in Guangdong Province.

                    In November 2023, the University of Basel‚Äôs Faculty of Theology awarded Ltuz an honorary doctorate. He returned to Switzerland in March 2024.

                    ‚Äî

                    1. Lee Ching Chee (Lee 2000; Alee 2013; Christian Weekly 1966a)

                      Lee Ching Chee (born 1933) was born in Hong Kong and fled to Guangzhou during the Japanese occupation, completing primary school there. She returned to Hong Kong in 1946, graduating from Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School in 1950 and from Grantham College of Education in 1953. She began her teaching career at St. Stephen‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College and, in 1954, returned to Ying Wa as a teacher after baptism in June.

                      In 1958-59, Lee studied theology at Selly Oak Colleges, Birmingham, before returning to Ying Wa for five more years of teaching. In 1964-66, she pursued theological studies again in the UK. Upon returning to Hong Kong, she was ordained in October 1966 as the first female pastor of the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China.

                      From 1966 to 1977, Lee served as the chaplain at Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School, also acting as vice-principal from 1972 to 1977. She represented the church at the World Council of Churches‚Äô Fourth Assembly, where she was elected to the Central Executive Committee. In 1977, she became the Mission Education Secretary for the Council for World Mission, visiting churches across Africa, India, the South Pacific, and Europe, including areas like South Africa, Northern Ireland, and Myanmar, often under curfews.

                      Returning to Hong Kong in 1981, Lee served as Deputy General Secretary of the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China and held leadership roles in its schools until her retirement in December 1997.

                      Lee was deeply involved in ecumenical work, serving in various leadership roles within the Hong Kong Christian Council from the 1960s onward. She published books including Half the Sky: The Status of Women in Churches (1993), Straightforward Words (1993), Collected Writings of Ching Chee (1998), and My Recollections (2000).

                      1981Âπ¥ÂõûÊ∏ØÂæåÔºå‰ªª‰∏≠ËèØÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÊúÉÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂçÄÊúÉÂâØÁ∏ΩÂππ‰∫ãÔºåÂÖºÂ±¨‰∏ã‰∏≠Â∞èÂ≠∏Ê†°Ëë£Ê†°Áõ£ËÅ∑ÔºåËá≥1997Âπ¥12ÊúàÂ∫ïÊ¶Æ‰ºë„ÄÇÁî±ÊñºÈï∑ÊúüÊúçÂãôÊïôÊúÉÂ≠∏Ê†°ÁöÑÁ∂ìÊ≠∑Ôºå‰ΩøÂ•πÂ∞çÊïôÊúÉÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÊïôËÇ≤ÊúâÊõ¥Ê∑±ÁöÑÈ´îÊúÉÔºå‰∏ªÂºµ‰ª•Ëá™Áî±Á©∫ÈñìËÄåÈùûÁ°¨Èä∑Âº∑Ëø´ÊñπÂºèÔºå‰æÜÊïôÂ∞éÂ≠∏Áîü„ÄÇÂêåÊôÇÔºåÂ∞çÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊïôÊúÉËæ¶Â≠∏ÁöÑÁêÜÂøµÂèäÁõÆÁöÑÔºåÂ•π‰πü‰ΩúÂá∫Ê∑±ÂàªÁöÑÂèçÊÄù„ÄÇ

                      ÊùéÁâßÂ∏´Á©çÊ•µÂèÉËàáÂêà‰∏Ä‰∫ãÂ∑•„ÄÇËá™1960Âπ¥‰ª£Ëµ∑ÔºåÂÖàÂæåÂ§öÊ¨°‰ªªÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂçîÈÄ≤ÊúÉÂü∑Ë°åÂßîÂì°ÊúÉÂßîÂì°„ÄÅÂâØ‰∏ªÂ∏≠ÂèäÂêà‰∏ÄÂßîÂì°ÊúÉ‰∏ªÂ∏≠Á≠âËÅ∑„ÄÇÊõæÂá∫Áâà„ÄäÂçäÈÇäÂ§©‚ÄîÂ©¶Â•≥Âú®ÊïôÊúÉÁöÑÂú∞‰Ωç„ÄãÔºà1993ÔºâÂèä„ÄäÊ∏ÖÂøÉÁõ¥Ë™™„ÄãÔºà1993Ôºâ„ÄÅ„ÄäÊ∏ÖË©ûÈõÜ„ÄãÔºà1998ÔºâÂèä„ÄäÊàëÊÜ∂ÊïÖÊàëÂú®„ÄãÔºà2000ÔºâÁ≠âÊõ∏„ÄÇ

                      1. David C. P. Lew (1929‚Äì1998)

                        (Lew, 1968, 1970, 1972; Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union, 1998; Lee, 2014: 99-102)

                        David C. P. Lew, born in Zhongshan, Guangdong, was originally a member of the Guangdong Chinese Christian Church Fujitang in Shanghai. He moved to Hong Kong following the regime change in China. In 1951, he dedicated himself to ministry during a summer camp held by the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China. In 1952, he enrolled at Trinity Theological College in Singapore and graduated in 1955. Ordained by the Church of Christ in China in 1959, he served as the pastor of Kowloon Church and held positions as vice-chairman and head of the publication department of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union. From 1968 to 1977, he served as the General Secretary of the Union.

                        David Lew was deeply concerned about social issues. After the 1967 riots in Hong Kong, he wrote an article titled New Directions for Churches in Hong Kong‚Äôs Rapidly Changing Society. In it, he criticized churches for becoming ‚Äúsmall organizations serving only their members‚Äô spiritual and physical needs,‚Äù while remaining indifferent to societal issues. He urged churches to address labor, housing, and unemployment issues, submit memorandums to the government, and advocate for a society that aligns more closely with the principles of the Kingdom of Heaven.

                        In a 1972 report as General Secretary of the Union, Lew again criticized churches for ignoring societal ‚Äúsins, darkness, and violence,‚Äù while also cautioning against abandoning gospel principles in the pursuit of social reform. He believed that both extremes failed to effectively witness to the truth of Christ. He emphasized the importance of expressing Christian views to the government while practicing the principle of ‚Äúbeing in the world but not of the world.‚Äù

                        Writing under the pen name ‚ÄúAmos‚Äù in the Christian Weekly, Lew authored the column Reflections at the City Gate. For instance, in 1970, he foresaw that Hong Kong‚Äôs era of reliance on overseas aid for refugees would become a thing of the past.

                        In 1975, when Billy Graham conducted his first evangelistic crusade in Hong Kong, Lew served as General Secretary of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union and as the campaign‚Äôs Executive Secretary.

                        After stepping down in 1977, he worked for Kirin Press before moving to Toronto in 1981, where he became the Senior Pastor of the Toronto Chinese United Church. In the early 1990s, he helped establish the Toronto Chinese Christian Churches Union. He retired in 1994 and passed away in January 1998.

                        ‚Äî

                        1. Yung Chi-tung (1908‚Äì1987)

                          (CUHK, 1987; Yung, 1960)

                          Yung Chi-tung, born in Zhuhai, Guangdong, and raised in Hong Kong, was the son of Yung Hing-kiu, one of the Chinese students sent to study in the United States during the Qing Dynasty under the program organized by Yung Wing. After graduating from Lingnan School in Guangzhou, Yung studied biology at Tsinghua University, earning his degree in 1929. He stayed on as a teaching assistant and later pursued advanced studies at the University of Chicago, where he earned his Ph.D. in 1937.

                          Returning to China, Yung taught at Northwest University and later at Lingnan University in Guangzhou, where he held various positions, including department chair, acting dean of the Faculty of Science, acting registrar, and acting president.

                          In 1951, Yung moved to Hong Kong and joined the Department of Biology at the University of Hong Kong. In 1959, he became a board member of Chung Chi College and was appointed its third president in 1960. During his tenure, Chung Chi College prepared to join The Chinese University of Hong Kong (CUHK). When CUHK was established in 1963, Chung Chi became a founding college, and Yung also served as CUHK‚Äôs vice-chancellor. Additionally, he became the founding dean of Chung Chi Theological Seminary in 1963.

                          Yung retired in 1975 and was named honorary president by the Chung Chi College Board. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1987.

                          Before Chung Chi joined CUHK, Yung emphasized that the newly established university would operate as a ‚Äúfederated university,‚Äù allowing member colleges to retain their autonomy. He believed that Chung Chi College, which means ‚Äúto revere Christ,‚Äù should carry forward the traditions of the 13 Christian universities in China while adapting to new circumstances. He sought to integrate Christian values within the university structure.

                          In recognition of his contributions to higher education in Hong Kong, Yung was awarded honorary Doctor of Laws degrees by the University of Hong Kong (1961) and CUHK (1975). He was also named a Fellow of the Royal Society for the Encouragement of Arts, Manufactures, and Commerce in 1972. In 2006, the new chapel building of Chung Chi Theological Seminary was named the ‚ÄúYung Chi-tung Memorial Building‚Äù in honor of his contributions to Christian education.

                          ‚Äî

                          1. Wu Ming Chieh (1916‚Äì1990)

                            (Wu Yu Kui, 1996: 22-26)

                            Wu Ming Chieh, born in Henan Province, became a Christian in 1943 and entered Hubei Shakou Lutheran Theological Seminary in 1946. In 1948, due to the Chinese Civil War, the seminary relocated to Tao Fong Shan in Hong Kong, where Wu arrived with faculty and students in December. After graduating in 1950, he served as an assistant preacher at the Lutheran Mandarin Church in Kowloon and was ordained as a pastor in November

  2. In 1952, Wu began mission work in Yuen Long, where he established a Lutheran Church.

    In 1959, he became president of the Lutheran Bible Seminary. In 1961, he was elected Supervisor of the Hong Kong Lutheran Church at its 8th General Assembly, a position he held through multiple terms, including in 1963, 1965, and 1969. Under his leadership, the church developed a seven-year plan for self-sufficiency, gradually reducing financial aid from overseas by one-seventh each year until achieving self-support in 1969.

    In 1968, the Lutheran Bible Seminary merged with another institution to form the Lutheran Theological Seminary, where Wu served as vice president. In 1970, he received an honorary Doctor of Theology degree from Pacific Lutheran University in the United States.

    During the 1970s, Wu continued his leadership roles, including representing the Lutheran Church in founding the Lutheran Theological Seminary of Hong Kong in collaboration with other denominations in 1976. He also chaired the organizing committee of the World Chinese Evangelical Fellowship in Hong Kong.

    In 1981, Wu retired as Supervisor but was named Honorary Lifetime Supervisor and Honorary Professor at the Lutheran Theological Seminary. He passed away in Hong Kong in September 1990.

    1. John Pao (1922‚Äì2013)

      (Pang 2014: 173-176)

      John Pao was born in Beijing in 1922 and converted to Christianity in

  3. In 1949, he pursued further theological education in the U.S., attending Wheaton College, Fuller Theological Seminary, Westminster Theological Seminary, and North Baptist Theological Seminary. He earned his Doctor of Theology in 1959 and was ordained as a pastor by the Evangelical Free Church of Wheaton that same year.

    In October 1959, Pao moved to Hong Kong, initially teaching at the Guangzhou Bible Institute. In 1961, he became the institute‚Äôs president. In 1965, the institute was renamed the Evangel Seminary, where Pao served as president until his retirement in 1985.

    Pao was dedicated to the service of the Evangelical Free Church of China, serving as chairman of the General Assembly (1971‚Äì1976) and as Supervisor from 1989 until his retirement in 1993, after which he was named Honorary Supervisor. In 1994, he moved to the U.S. to serve as president of the Christ Workers Theological Seminary in California, a position he held until 2000. In 2003, he returned to Hong Kong at the invitation of the Evangel Seminary to serve as ‚ÄúSpecial Appointed President‚Äù for a year.

    Pao authored several books, including The Armor of Truth, The Equipment of Life, and commentaries on Colossians, Romans, and Revelation, as well as Theology of the Bible and Christology. Between 1960 and 1995, he served as a speaker at the Hong Kong and Kowloon Bible Study and Evangelistic Conference ten times.

    1. Chan Hay Him (1934‚Äì2019)

      (Chan 2015; Fellowship of Evangelical Students, 2019)

      Chan Hay Him was born in Jieyang County, Guangdong, into a Christian family. At the age of 16, he moved to Hong Kong alone and attended Pui Ching Middle School. In July 1951, he experienced spiritual revival during a summer camp hosted by the Hong Kong Baptist Youth Fellowship. In 1954, during another camp, he responded to a call to ministry. After graduating from high school in 1955, he enrolled at the Hong Kong Baptist Theological Seminary and began attending and serving at the Hong Kong Chiu Chow Baptist Church.

      After graduating in 1960, Chan dedicated himself to student evangelism. In 1961, he became the first General Secretary of the Fellowship of Evangelical Students in Hong Kong, a position he held until 1974. In 1973, he pursued further studies in missions at Fuller Theological Seminary in the U.S., earning a Master‚Äôs degree. Chan also helped initiate the Breakthrough movement, suggesting its name.

      In 1975, Chan became the Student Counselor and Missions Lecturer at the China Graduate School of Theology. From 1977 to 1980, he served as General Secretary of Ambassadors for Christ in the U.S. and was ordained as a pastor in New York in November 1978. He later served as Senior Pastor at North York Chinese Baptist Church in Toronto, Canada (1980‚Äì1986, 1997‚Äì2000). From 1986 to 1996, he held two terms as General Secretary of the Chinese Coordination Centre of World Evangelism.

      Returning to Hong Kong in 2000, Chan served as Senior Pastor of the West Point Chiu Chow Baptist Church until his retirement in 2008. His publications include Why Doesn‚Äôt the Church Measure Up? (2008) and In These Years: God‚Äôs Revival Work and the Student Evangelism Movement (2015).

      ‚Äî

      1. Wong Tsok (1922‚Äì1995)

        (Hing 2014: 51-54)

        Wong Tsok, born in Xinhui, Guangdong, in March 1922, grew up in a rural non-Christian family. At age 11, he moved to Foshan, where he joined the Sunday School at the Wen Chang Sha Church of the Methodist Church, cultivating his Christian faith. He later became a Sunday School teacher. In 1940, he was baptized by Rev. Frank White.

        In 1944, Wong committed to becoming a pastor and enrolled at the Union Theological College of Lingnan University in Guangzhou. After graduating in 1948, he was assigned to the Hong Kong Methodist Church. Between 1948 and 1951, he served as pastor of the Kowloon Circuit and was responsible for constructing the new Kowloon Church and establishing a school.

        In 1952, Wong was formally accepted as a Methodist minister. However, due to political shifts, the Hong Kong Methodist Circuit declared its independence from British imperialism in 1951 and operated under the name ‚ÄúChinese Methodist Church in Hong Kong Circuit‚Äù until 1955, when Rev. Shearer officiated the ordination.

        In 1959, the Hong Kong Circuit was reorganized as the Hong Kong District under the British Methodist Mission, with Wong serving as District Superintendent. He became Chairman of the Hong Kong District in 1964, overseeing the construction of the North Point Church (1966) and discussing a union with the Methodist Church of China. When the Hong Kong Methodist Church was officially formed in 1975, Wong was elected as its first President, serving two terms until 1982.

        Wong was a strong advocate for Christian education, overseeing the establishment of several schools, including Methodist School (1951), Methodist College (1958), North Point Methodist School (1964), and Wa Ying College (1971).

        In 1984, Wong was awarded an MBE (Member of the Order of the British Empire) by the Hong Kong government in recognition of his contributions to education and religion. Wong retired in 1987 and passed away on September 26, 1995.

        ‚Äî

        1. Jackie Pullinger (1944‚Äì)

          (St. Stephen‚Äôs Society; Pullinger & Grieg, 1996)

          Jackie Pullinger was born in the United Kingdom in 1944. At age five, she decided she wanted to become a missionary. After graduating from the Royal College of Music, her first job was teaching music in England. However, she continued to feel a call to missionary work.

          In 1965, Pullinger sought opportunities to serve in Hong Kong, but her attempts to secure a position as a music teacher or through churches failed. Undeterred, she arrived in Hong Kong alone in 1966.

          Pullinger found work teaching music at a kindergarten in the Kowloon Walled City, a lawless area notorious for drug trafficking and triad activity. She established the Kowloon Walled City Youth Club, offering activities such as table tennis and camping trips for local youth. Gradually, young people accepted the gospel message, but many were not welcomed by local churches due to their appearance and gang affiliations.

          Pullinger began hosting gatherings and even housing addicts in her home, where she helped them overcome addiction through spiritual prayer. Despite facing hostility from triads, she expanded her work, establishing several counseling centers and founding the St. Stephen‚Äôs Society in 1974.

          In 1985, the Hong Kong government allowed her to set up ‚ÄúHome of Joy‚Äù in temporary housing, where marginalized groups, including addicts and the elderly, could worship and dine together. She also worked with Vietnamese refugees and managed a refugee camp upon the invitation of the UNHCR in 1994.

          Pullinger received an MBE (Member of the Order of the British Empire) in 1988 for her contributions to public welfare. In 2023, the University of Hong Kong awarded her an honorary Doctor of Social Sciences in recognition of her passionate service to society.

          (10) ÊΩòÂçìÈùàJackie PullingerÔºàËÅñÂ£´ÊèêËä¨ÊúÉndÔºõÊΩòÂçìÈùà„ÄÅÊ°ÇÁàæÂÖã1996Ôºâ

          ÊΩòÂçìÈùàÔºàJackie Pullinger, 1944-ÔºâÔºåÁîüÊñºËã±ÂúãÔºå5Ê≠≤ÂπºÂ∞è‰πãÈΩ°‰æøÁ´ãÂøóË¶ÅÂÅöÂÇ≥ÊïôÂ£´„ÄÇÂæûÁöáÂÆ∂Èü≥Ê®ÇÂ≠∏Èô¢Áï¢Ê•≠ÂæåÔºåÊΩòÂçìÈùàÁöÑÁ¨¨‰∏Ä‰ªΩÂ∑•‰ΩúÊòØÂú®Ëã±ÂúãÊïôÈü≥Ê®ÇÔºå‰ΩÜÂ•π‰∏¶Ê≤°ÊúâÊîæÊ£ÑÂÇ≥ÈÅìÊïô‰∫∫ÁöÑÂëºÂè¨„ÄÇ1965Âπ¥ÔºåÂ•πÂòóË©¶Â∞ãÊâæÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÂØ¶Ë∏êÁêÜÊÉ≥ÁöÑÈÄîÂæëÔºåÂèØÊÉúÂ•πÂêëÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊîøÂ∫úÊ±ÇËÅ∑Áï∂Â∞àÊ•≠Èü≥Ê®ÇÂ∏´ÂíåÂêëÊïôÊúÉÊ±ÇËÅ∑ÈÉΩÂëäÂ§±Êïó„ÄÇ‰∏çÈÅéÔºåÂ•π‰ªçÂú®1966Âπ¥ÈöªË∫´‰æÜÂà∞È¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ

          ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

          (Hong Kong Christian Population)

          (Noren, 1964; Hing 2024: 199)

          Loren E. Noren of the American Baptist Foreign Mission Society conducted the third post-war systematic survey of church growth in Hong Kong, covering the period from 1958 to 1962. His report, Urban Church Growth in Hong Kong 1958-1962, published in 1964, revealed significant growth in Hong Kong churches. By 1962, there were 344 churches and 112,200 Christians, marking a 111.7% increase in membership since 1955, with an average annual growth rate of 15.9%.

          In 1962, eight denominations had over 5,000 members each: Baptists (54 churches, 15,585 members), the Church of Christ in China (24 churches, 14,367 members), Anglicans (23 churches, 13,697 members), Lutherans (33 churches, 10,801 members), Evangelical Lutheran Church (18 churches, 9,597 members), Methodists (8 churches, 7,782 members), United Brethren (6 churches, 5,402 members), and Tsung Tsin Mission (11 churches, 5,069 members). Together, these eight denominations accounted for 51% of all churches and 73% of the total Christian population in Hong Kong.

          In 1970, at the Consultation on Church Development and Cooperative Planning organized by the Hong Kong Christian Council, Noren presented updated figures. By 1965, there were 423 churches with 135,016 members. By 1968, these numbers had grown to 470 churches and 159,359 members, representing a 29.6% increase in Christian population over six years.

          ‚Äî

          1. Christian Industrial Committee

            Hong Kong‚Äôs economic development during the 1960s was closely tied to industrialization. However, the rights of hundreds of thousands of laborers were often neglected. With Christians comprising a small percentage of the working class, the Hong Kong Christian Council (HKCC) recognized the need to address labor issues. In September 1959, the HKCC established the Christian Industrial Committee (CIC) as a subcommittee.

            In 1966, the HKCC requested assistance from the World Council of Churches, leading to the arrival of Margaret Kane. Under her leadership, the CIC established its objectives, strategies, and guidelines. The committee introduced ‚ÄúLabor Sunday‚Äù in 1966, producing prayer materials and worship booklets to raise awareness of labor issues within churches.

            After the 1967 riots, the CIC‚Äôs vision became clearer. Initially focusing on ‚Äúindustrial chaplaincy,‚Äù the committee, under the leadership of Fung Wai-man starting in 1969, shifted its approach to advocating for workers‚Äô rights, adopting a stance of ‚Äústanding with the workers.‚Äù

            ‚Äî

            1. Fellowship of Evangelical Students (FES), 1961

              (Fellowship of Evangelical Students, 2017: 2-25; Kwok, 2009: 125-152)

              In 1956, David H. Adeney, a British missionary who had promoted student evangelism in China, accepted an invitation from the International Fellowship of Evangelical Students (IFES) to serve as Regional Secretary for East Asia in Hong Kong. Adeney first revitalized the University of Hong Kong Christian Association (established in 1953) and later formed the Graduate Christian Fellowship (GCF) in 1957. That same year, Adeney and GCF members established the Evangelical Reading Room to promote Christian literature among students, becoming a hub for student evangelism.

              By 1960, Christian students at Grantham College of Education established the Teacher Christian Fellowship (TCF). These efforts culminated in the official founding of the Fellowship of Evangelical Students (FES) in October 1961. In 1963, the Inter-College Christian Fellowship (ICCF) was formed, later renamed the Tertiary Evangelical Fellowship in 1971, fostering collaboration between Christian fellowships at institutions such as Chung Chi College, Baptist College, New Asia College, and the University of Hong Kong.

              The FES introduced Western evangelical literature, such as works by J. I. Packer and John Stott, broadening the theological perspectives of Christian students. Adeney promoted systematic Bible study, using thematic, book-based, or historical approaches, which enriched students‚Äô rational understanding of faith. Free from denominational constraints, FES also experimented with creative evangelistic methods, incorporating folk songs, traditional dances, and social activities like tea gatherings and travel, appealing to young people and breaking away from conservative church traditions.

              The student evangelical movement of the 1950s and 1960s nurtured many future church leaders and indirectly inspired movements such as Breakthrough, the China Graduate School of Theology, and the Industrial Evangelism Movement.

              ‚Äî

              1. Chung Chi Theological Seminary, 1963

                (Hing 2018a)

                The primary goal of theological education is to train church workers. Post-war Hong Kong experienced a ‚Äúgolden era‚Äù of theological education, with 14 seminaries operating in the late 1950s. However, a report on theological education in Southeast Asia noted that many of Hong Kong‚Äôs seminaries were denominational and lacked advanced theological training.

                Bishop Ralph A. Ward of the Methodist Church observed in the early 1950s that Hong Kong churches could no longer rely on seminaries in China to train pastors. He advocated for the establishment of a union theological seminary modeled after cooperative seminaries in China. Bishop Hall, a key figure in Chung Chi College‚Äôs founding, expressed his vision for a theological seminary in 1954 during the dedication of Ho Fuk Tong Hall.

                In 1956, Chung Chi College introduced Christian education courses, reorganizing the Department of Sociology and Education into the Department of Religious Education and Social Work. By 1958, plans were underway to establish a Department of Theology and Religious Education.

                In 1963, Chung Chi College became a founding member of The Chinese University of Hong Kong (CUHK). To better serve the pastoral training needs of local churches, the college established the Chung Chi Theological Seminary in the same year. Initially independent of CUHK, the seminary remained affiliated with Chung Chi College, respecting its Christian tradition while enjoying greater autonomy.

                By 1967, the seminary was reintegrated into CUHK‚Äôs structure, merging with the Department of Religious Knowledge and Philosophy to form the Department of Philosophy and Religion. The seminary became the Theology Division within the department, maintaining academic independence while relying on external funding.

                ‚Äî

                1. Evangelical School Development Incorporation (ESDI), 1964

                  (Wong 2024: 161-163; Carmel Secondary School, 1965)

                  In 1964, Dr. Kim Shing-yu, a professor of mechanical and electrical engineering at the University of Hong Kong, founded the Evangelical School Development Incorporation (ESDI) to establish schools with sound evangelical faith and high educational standards.

                  Born in Shanghai in 1919, Kim studied at the University of Hong Kong during the Sino-Japanese War, graduating in 1940. After relocating to Guilin during the Japanese occupation, he later received a scholarship to study in the UK, earning a doctorate from Imperial College London in

  4. Upon returning to Hong Kong in 1948, he joined the University of Hong Kong‚Äôs engineering faculty.

    The first school established by ESDI was Carmel Secondary School, a private institution in Ho Man Tin. Initially borrowing the facilities of Carmel Village Life School, its name commemorates the biblical Mount Carmel, where the prophet Elijah defeated false prophets.

    Carmel Secondary School‚Äôs first principal, Cheung Tsz-keung, actively promoted evangelism within the school. In 1979, the Carmel Church was established to cater to students whose families opposed church attendance, allowing them to worship on school premises.

    Since the late 1970s, ESDI has established several secondary and primary schools, including Carmel Pak U Secondary School (1979), Carmel Leung Sing Tak School (1979), Carmel Alison Lam Foundation Secondary School (1982), and Carmel Divine Grace Foundation Secondary School (1998). All schools emphasize student evangelism, requiring teachers to be Christians committed to sharing the gospel.

    1. Hong Kong Christian Service (1967)

      (Hing 2018b: 36)

      By the late 1960s, as the refugee situation in Hong Kong stabilized, overseas donations began to decline. A survey by the Hong Kong Council of Social Service indicated that in the mid-1960s, 59% of voluntary organization funds came from overseas sources. However, this percentage decreased steadily, dropping to 38% in the 1969-70 fiscal year, 33% in 1970-71, and 30% in 1971-72. Many international welfare organizations began to reassess Hong Kong as no longer needing emergency relief, shifting their focus to Vietnam and Southeast Asia.

      In this context, international Christian service organizations also started withdrawing from Hong Kong. On May 1967, the Hong Kong Christian Welfare & Relief Council merged with the Church World Service to form the Hong Kong Christian Service, which became an affiliate of the Hong Kong Christian Council. With the departure of international Christian relief and service organizations, Christian social services in Hong Kong had to undergo a transformation. Local funding sources, such as the Lotteries Fund (established in 1965) and the Community Chest (operational since 1968), became increasingly important in sustaining social service programs, ushering in a new era of local Christian social work.

      ‚Äî

      1. Evangelical Centre Movement

        (Chan 2015: 58-137; Wong 2003)

        In the early 1960s, divisions in four Baptist churches led to the establishment of independent ‚ÄúEvangelical Centres.‚Äù These included Kowloon City Evangelical Centre (founded in April 1961 after a split from Kowloon City Baptist Church), Bonham Road Evangelical Centre (founded in September 1961 after a split from Caine Road Baptist Church), Shau Kei Wan Evangelical Centre (founded in 1962 after a split from Shau Kei Wan Baptist Church), and Ping On Evangelical Centre (founded in 1962 after a split from Po On Road Baptist Church).

        These splits reflected underlying tensions between younger members and church leadership, stemming from the 1950s Baptist Youth Fellowship revival movement. The Baptist Youth Fellowship was an independent organization of youth groups from various Baptist churches, holding seasonal meetings and large summer camps with hundreds of participants, primarily from key churches like Caine Road, Tsim Sha Tsui, and Kowloon City (Satirun Road).

        Following the revival, young members increasingly expressed dissatisfaction with the existing church systems, while church leaders viewed them as rebellious. In 1957, the Baptist Convention‚Äôs youth division proposed replacing youth fellowships with ‚ÄúYoung Adult Fellowships,‚Äù a move resisted by youth leaders. This worsened tensions, leading to a ‚Äúbattle‚Äù mentality among young members. In 1959, the Baptist Convention held a seminar to discuss disbanding the Youth Fellowship, ultimately resulting in splits in several Baptist churches during the 1960s.

        Ping On Evangelical Centre was formed under unique circumstances. Dissatisfied with a pastor at Po On Road Baptist Church, members such as Ng Chuk-kwong and Chan Sik-on began meeting independently for Bible study and evangelism. They initially hesitated to leave their church but eventually formed an independent fellowship in October 1962. By December, they rented a space in a wooden hut for regular gatherings, naming it Ping On Evangelical Centre (later renamed Sham Shui Po Ping On Evangelical Centre in 1969).

        Ping On Evangelical Centre expanded rapidly, founding new congregations such as Tsim Sha Tsui Ping On Evangelical Centre (1969), Tai Kok Tsui Ping On Evangelical Centre (1976), and Kowloon City Ping On Evangelical Centre (1977). By 1988, there were 18 Ping On Evangelical Centres across Hong Kong, growing to 32 by 2002. The ministry extended from evangelism to education (e.g., Sha Tin Kindergarten in 1990, Tsing Yi Kindergarten in 2002, and Ping On Evangelical Secondary School in

  1. and social services (e.g., Fu Shan Social Service Centre in 1995).

    Ping On Evangelical Centre also established an organizational structure to support its growth. In 1970, it began publishing the Ping On Monthly Bulletin. In 1978, it initiated fellowship meetings for co-workers, and in 1982, it formed the Evangelical Ministry Committee. By 1988, it had launched a short-term mission training center, followed by a pastoral training institute in 1995.

    1. Christian Anti-Gambling Movement

      (Hing 2018b: 41-42)

      In 1960, a proposal to introduce football betting in Hong Kong prompted the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union to send a letter of strong opposition to the Governor and Legislative Council members. In 1964, the union actively opposed the establishment of a greyhound racing track and the legalization of illegal horse betting. Various Christian organizations, including the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union, the Hong Kong Christian Council, the YWCA, and the Hong Kong Christian Welfare and Relief Council, expressed firm opposition. Their advocacy pressured the government to heed public opinion and refrain from introducing new forms of gambling beyond those already permitted.

      In 1967, the union opposed a proposal by the New Territories Heung Yee Kuk to establish a greyhound racing track in the New Territories, further underscoring its consistent anti-gambling stance.

      ‚Äî

      1. Inauguration of Christian Weekly

        (Ng 2014: 108-112)

        In October 1963, the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union established a publication department led by Wong Tsok (Methodist Church) to prepare for the launch of Christian Weekly. The inaugural issue was published on August 30, 1964, featuring sections such as church news, sermons, family and women‚Äôs issues, youth topics, and children‚Äôs stories. The first print run of 30,000 copies was distributed to churches across Hong Kong for promotion.

        In the foreword, Wong Tsok articulated the paper‚Äôs mission: (1) reporting Christian news, (2) nurturing spiritual growth, and (3) spreading Christian truths. Editorial guidelines included:

        1. Content must relate to Christianity and align with the Bible and the union‚Äôs constitution.

          1. Avoid political involvement.

            1. Refrain from attacking other religions, denominations, organizations, or individuals.

              1. Avoid emphasizing denominational distinctions.

                1. Promote truth and oppose sin.

                  1. Prioritize constructive content.

                    1. Correct errors in citations, grammar, and plagiarism.

                      Christian Weekly was Hong Kong‚Äôs first weekly Christian publication for all churches, serving as a platform for expressing the union‚Äôs views. Special issues addressed significant topics such as the ‚ÄúAnti-Corruption Movement‚Äù (1977), ‚ÄúAnti-Pornography Campaign‚Äù (1979), ‚ÄúOpposition to Legalizing Homosexuality‚Äù (1981), and ‚ÄúOpposition to Expanding Jockey Club Betting Shops‚Äù (1983).

                      ‚Äî

                      1. Hong Kong Christian Response to the 1966 Riots

                        In response to the Kowloon disturbances triggered by fare increases on the Star Ferry, Rev. David C. P. Lew of the Church of Christ in China wrote a commentary titled Opposition to Price Hikes! Opposition to Riots! in the April 24, 1966, issue of Christian Weekly. Lew acknowledged the motives of the peaceful protesters but lamented the violence and looting that ensued, which he deemed contrary to Christ‚Äôs teachings of peace. His reflections included:

                        1. All forms of violence are wrong.

                          1. Opposition to price hikes is justified.

                            1. Public sentiment should be taken seriously.

                              1. Education policies must be reviewed, as the riots exposed issues among Hong Kong‚Äôs youth, including the shortcomings of the education system and its neglect of moral and social awareness.

                                On May 15, Christian Weekly published Ho Tin-yau‚Äôs article The Church Cannot Ignore Social Issues Facing Youth, which emphasized the seriousness of youth-related issues revealed by the riots. Ho urged churches to adopt a prophetic stance to address youth delinquency and to strengthen youth ministry through publications, Sunday schools, recreation centers, and industrial evangelism.

                                On May 22, Paul R. Webb of the Hong Kong Christian Welfare Union attributed the riots to changes over the preceding two decades, including population growth, industrial development, materialism, inadequate child education, and existential uncertainty among Hong Kong residents due to unclear political status. He urged churches to take responsibility for addressing these societal issues.

                                Overall, the Christian response mirrored the government‚Äôs inquiry into the riots, recognizing deeper societal and youth-related issues.

                                ‚Äî

                                1. Hong Kong Christian Response to the 1967 Riots

                                  (Hing 2018b: 82-86)

                                  Initially sparked by labor strikes, the 1967 riots led to early Christian responses focusing on labor disputes. The Christian Industrial Committee of the Hong Kong Christian Council called for an independent investigation into the strikes and emphasized the need for all parties to prioritize societal welfare.

                                  As the unrest escalated, Anglican Bishop Gilbert Baker issued a pastoral letter on May 8, urging citizens to uphold ‚Äúpeace and order‚Äù and calling for mutual responsibility between employers and employees. He also stressed the church‚Äôs role in addressing youth issues and building a harmonious society.

                                  On May 22, the Hong Kong Christian Council Executive Committee issued a public appeal for peace and social order, discouraging youth from participating in disruptive actions. The Church of Christ in China‚Äôs Hong Kong Council also issued a statement, lamenting the violence and urging the government to address labor disputes and youth issues.

                                  The 1968-69 annual report of the Hong Kong Christian Council reflected on the riots, noting a renewed church focus on societal dynamics and a stronger commitment to addressing social challenges, particularly those affecting youth.

                                  ‚Äî

                                  1. EACC‚Äôs Faith and Order Conference, 1966

                                    (Christian Weekly, 1966b, 1966c, 1966d)

                                    The East Asia Christian Conference (EACC) held its first Faith and Order Consultation in Hong Kong from October 26 to November 3, 1966, at the Methodist Church‚Äôs Kowloon Chapel. The conference, themed Confessing Christianity in Asia Today, featured speakers such as Huang Tsang Hui, Visser ‚Äôt Hooft, and D. T. Niles.

                                    The conference issued a declaration emphasizing the ‚Äúindependence and autonomy‚Äù of Asian churches and the importance of interpreting God‚Äôs work within Asian contexts, avoiding reliance on Western doctrinal frameworks. This marked the beginning of ‚Äúconfessing theology,‚Äù which laid the foundation for the development of Asian Theology in the 1970s.

                                    The EACC, established in 1957 in Indonesia, was renamed the Christian Conference of Asia in 1973.

                                    ‚Äî

                                    1. Ecumenical Practices Between Christianity and Catholicism

                                      (Fu 2018: 101-106, 110)

                                      The ecumenical movement between Hong Kong Christians and Catholics was significantly influenced by the Second Vatican Council (1962-65). Before the council, cooperation was primarily non-religious. For instance, in 1960, the Catholic Diocese joined Protestant groups, such as the Anglican Church and the Methodist Church, on the RTHK Chinese Religious Broadcasting Advisory Committee to produce programs.

                                      Post-Vatican II, ecumenical dialogue intensified. In 1966, the Catholic Diocese established the Christian Unity Committee and introduced annual Week of Prayer for Christian Unity services. Joint Bible study and prayer meetings were organized, and in 1969, clergy from both traditions began exchanging pulpits.

                                      In 1968, the Catholic Diocese and the Hong Kong Bible Society explored producing a jointly approved Chinese Bible translation. However, disagreements over terminology (e.g., ‚ÄúGod‚Äù vs. ‚ÄúLord‚Äù) ultimately ended the collaboration. Despite this, ecumenical efforts continued, fostering greater mutual understanding and cooperation.

                                      1. Tensions Between Churches and Student Fellowships

                                        (Hing 2004: 168-169)

                                        Evangelical student movements that began in the late 1950s were not widely accepted by mainstream denominational churches. Many churches viewed student ministries as disruptive to denominational unity. Events like the Evangelical Centre Movement in the early 1960s and the St. Paul‚Äôs Seven incident further reinforced these perceptions. Church leaders often blamed student fellowships for church splits.

                                        For example, in the case of the youth group at Caine Road Baptist Church, young members who left to establish Bonham Road Evangelical Centre used the Evangelical Reading Room as a base for prayer and Bible study, inviting pastors such as Lee Fei-wu and John Chan to guide them. Evangelical leader Stephen Tong acknowledged that students often left churches due to dissatisfaction, with some bringing pride into churches without contributing but expecting leadership roles or being overly critical. This strained the relationship between student fellowships and churches.

                                        From the church perspective, youth engagement in student ministries often came at the expense of local church service, representing a significant loss. Some church leaders, unable to adopt a broader perspective of the ‚Äúinvisible church,‚Äù sought to limit youth involvement in campus fellowships, viewing such activities as detrimental to the local congregation.

                                        ‚Äî

                                        1. Social Responsibility of Christianity

                                          The 1966 and 1967 riots significantly impacted Hong Kong society, prompting reflection within the Hong Kong Christian Council (HKCC). In its 1969 work review, the council noted that improvements were needed in both political and economic organization to create a more humane society. However, churches had largely neglected their call to contribute to societal welfare (Hing 2018b: 42).

                                          The influence of fundamentalist traditions meant that many Chinese churches in Hong Kong remained unaware of their social responsibilities. Historical debates between fundamentalism and liberal theology further deepened this divide, with fundamentalists often rejecting social concern as part of the ‚Äúsocial gospel‚Äù or labeling it as modernist theology or unbelief.

                                          Stephen Tong was among the first evangelical pastors in Hong Kong to reflect on the isolationist tendencies of fundamentalism. Speaking at the 1966 Congress on the Church‚Äôs Worldwide Mission in Wheaton, Illinois, Tong emphasized that while the primary mission of the church was to evangelize and save souls, acts of loving service, such as education, healthcare, and welfare, also held value in evangelism. However, he maintained a hierarchical view, prioritizing evangelism over social responsibility. This stance became a mainstream narrative within Chinese evangelical circles (Hing 2018b: 165-166).

                                          ‚Äî

                                          1. Achieving Self-Sufficiency and Autonomy

                                            From the 1960s, various mission organizations began planning for the financial and organizational independence of local churches.

                                            • Christian and Missionary Alliance (C&MA):

                                              In 1959, the C&MA initiated a five-year self-sufficiency plan. Starting in 1960, subsidies from Western missions to local churches were reduced by 20% annually, aiming for full self-sufficiency by 1964. By 1963, 10 C&MA churches in Hong Kong achieved financial independence, while nine newly established congregations were still reliant on external support (Ho 2021: 287-290).

                                              • Lutheran Church ‚Äì Hong Kong Synod:

                                                Established in 1954 with support from eight mission organizations, the Hong Kong Synod reached a self-support agreement in 1962. Under this agreement, subsidies from missions were reduced by one-seventh annually, ceasing entirely by the end of 1970 (Wu 1962: 3).

                                                • Church of Christ in China (CCC):

                                                  Starting in 1964, the CCC announced a phased reduction of foreign financial support, excluding social welfare work. Subsidies for local congregations and schools were decreased by 10% annually, with full self-sufficiency targeted by 1974. Congregations were also required to increase their contributions to the district council by 10% annually based on 1963 levels (Wang 1963: 5).

                                                  These measures reflected a broader movement toward autonomy and sustainability, marking a significant transition in the financial and administrative structure of Hong Kong churches.

                                                    1. Anti-Christian Movements During the 1967 Riots

                                                      (Hing 2018b: 76-81; Ying 2022: 7-47)

                                                      During the 1967 riots, Hong Kong‚Äôs churches were not directly targeted by protesters, and there were no organized campaigns against them. However, church-affiliated schools became a focal point. From June onward, students from numerous Catholic and Protestant schools were mobilized to form ‚Äústruggle committees‚Äù or ‚Äúcombat teams.‚Äù Reports in Ta Kung Pao between May and December documented over 50 church schools whose students were involved to varying degrees in the red movement.

                                                      The schools most affected included:

                                                      • Catholic Schools (21 schools): Notable examples were Kowloon Wah Yan College, St. Mary‚Äôs Canossian College, St. Joseph‚Äôs College, Sacred Heart Canossian College, La Salle College, and Maryknoll Convent School.

                                                        • Anglican Schools (10 schools): Schools included Bishop Hall Jubilee School, St. Stephen‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College, St. Mark‚Äôs School, Diocesan Boys‚Äô School, and Diocesan Girls‚Äô School.

                                                          • Church of Christ in China Schools (6 schools): These included Ying Wa College, Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School, and Hoh Fuk Tong College.

                                                            • Baptist and Tsung Tsin Schools (3 and 2 schools, respectively):

                                                              Other schools affiliated with Methodists, the Christian and Missionary Alliance, Lutherans, YMCA, and independent denominations were also implicated.

                                                              Even Christian higher education institutions, such as Hong Kong Baptist College and Chung Chi College, established struggle committees during the unrest.

                                                              Student Activities and Anti-Religious Sentiment

                                                              Struggle committees in church schools engaged in activities such as issuing statements, posting slogans, distributing leaflets, and even setting off firecrackers. Students from different schools often coordinated, participating in larger gatherings organized by the student struggle committee. On October 29, a united accusation assembly was held, specifically involving students and workers from church schools. At the assembly, attendees criticized church school administrators for ‚Äúusing religion to harm people,‚Äù claiming that ‚Äúsalvation does not come from Jesus or Mary, but from Mao Zedong Thought.‚Äù

                                                              Propaganda Through Youth Paradise

                                                              Before the riots, the leftist literary publication Youth Paradise served as a covert platform for underground organizations to influence young people. During the riots, it became a tool for promoting leftist ideologies, calling for resistance against colonial rule and aligning students with patriotic and revolutionary values. The publication also featured anti-religious rhetoric.

                                                              For instance, some church school students contributed articles to Youth Paradise that challenged religious beliefs. One writer dismissed God as a form of escapism, claiming that ‚Äúrevolutionary storms‚Äù required the rejection of ‚Äúworld-denying ideologies‚Äù to create a better future. From an atheistic perspective, another article accused religion of being a tool for exploitation and deception, describing theism as ‚Äúsuperstitious‚Äù and ‚Äúabsurd.‚Äù Religion, the writer argued, served to numb people and divert them from class struggles, asserting that ‚Äúthe creator of the world is not God, but the glorious and sacred laborers.‚Äù

                                                              Marxist Critique of Religion and Its Application in Hong Kong

                                                              The Marxist critique of religion was extensively employed during this period. Students linked Christianity in Hong Kong to colonial oppression, asserting that theology was used to perpetuate the ruling class‚Äôs agenda. They argued that Christian education indoctrinated students, portraying God as a tool of oppression and religion as a ‚Äúpoison‚Äù that exploited and enslaved the populace.

                                                              This rhetoric mirrored the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs (CCP) ‚Äúcultural invasion‚Äù narrative, which criticized church schools as instruments of colonialism and sought to expose the supposed collusion between Christianity and imperialist powers. By targeting church-affiliated schools, leftist propaganda aimed to delegitimize Christianity as complicit in colonial exploitation while mobilizing youth to reject religious and colonial ideologies.
